    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="2a13fea33ded401dbe1c0213d1c6cd16">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: '924a26da-2881-4959-8de9-1904efd21812'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" id="abstract-graphical-en">
         <h2 id="d84983807" class="article-section__header section__title graphical abstractlang_en graphical">Graphical Abstract</h2>
         <div class="graphical-abstract">
            <p>Hexanol hexaazatrinaphthalene, an organic electrode material with synergetic coupling effects of abundant redox-active sites and stable conjugated cores, via nine-electron transfer mechanism, delivers excellent rate capability, and robust cycling performance in sodium-ion batteries.
            </p><figure>
               <div class="center graphic__image"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/195e8fb9-729d-401e-b37d-2ffd6e69e128/anie202311460-toc-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/195e8fb9-729d-401e-b37d-2ffd6e69e128/anie202311460-toc-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/fe3c4a7c-0de9-4f4f-bb57-47860f4a6aa7/anie202311460-toc-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/195e8fb9-729d-401e-b37d-2ffd6e69e128/anie202311460-toc-0001-m.jpg" alt="Description unavailable" title="Description unavailable" loading="lazy"></picture></a><p class="caption-style"></p>
               </div>
            </figure>
            <p></p>
            </div>
      </section>
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d84983812" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Organic electrode materials (OEMs), valued for their sustainability and structural tunability, have been attracting increasing attention for wide application in sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) and other rechargeable batteries. However, most OEMs are plagued with insufficient specific capacity or poor cycling stability. Therefore, it′s imperative to enhance their specific capacity and cycling stability through molecular design. Herein, we designed and synthesized a heteroaromatic molecule 2,3,8,9,14,15-hexanol hexaazatrinaphthalene (HATN-6OH) by the synergetic coupling of catechol (the precursor of <i>ortho</i>-quinone)/<i>ortho</i>-quinone functional groups and HATN conjugated core structures. The abundance of catechol/<i>ortho</i>-quinone and imine redox-active moieties delivers a high specific capacity of nine-electron transfer for SIBs. Most notably, the π–π interactions and intermolecular hydrogen bond forces among HATN-6OH molecules secure the stable long-term cycling performance of SIBs. Consequently, the as-prepared HATN-6OH electrode exhibited a high specific capacity (554 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup>), excellent rate capability (202 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 10 A g<sup>−1</sup>), and stable long-term cycling performance (73 % after 3000 cycles at 10 A g<sup>−1</sup>) in SIBs. Additionally, the nine-electron transfer mechanism is confirmed by systematic density functional theory (DFT) calculation, attenuated total reflection Fourier transform infrared spectroscopy (ATR-FTIR), and Raman analysis. The achievement of the synergetic coupling of the redox-active sites on OEMs could be an important key to the enhancement of SIBs and other metal-ion batteries.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="anie202311460-sec-0001">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="anie202311460-sec-0001-title"> Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have been considered promising energy storage systems for many advantages, such as abundant sodium reserves and low cost.<span><sup><a href="#anie202311460-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202311460-bib-0001_R_d84983799e543" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1">1</a></sup></span> However, most inorganic electrode materials for SIBs are derived from non-renewable sources such as ores or require high-temperature calcination treatment, which may deplete over the long term, or the process could exacerbate energy consumption.<span><sup><a href="#anie202311460-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202311460-bib-0004_R_d84983799e546" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">2</a></sup></span> More importantly, there are rarely electrode materials that simultaneously possess high specific capacity, excellent rate capability, and good cycling stability. Hence, it becomes critically essential to develop novel, renewable, and green electrode materials to concurrently enhance various performance parameters, such as specific capacity, rate capability, and cycling stability.</p>
         
         <p>Organic electrode materials (OEMs) are promising candidates due to their unique merits, such as lightweight, structural flexibility, ample reserves, and good eco-friendliness.<span><sup><a href="#anie202311460-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202311460-bib-0005_R_d84983799e552" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3">3</a></sup></span> However, most OEMs could not simultaneously possess the high specific capacity and long cycling stability.<span><sup><a href="#anie202311460-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202311460-bib-0008_R_d84983799e555" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4">4</a></sup></span> Several strategies have been put up with to address the poor cycling stability of OEMs, such as salification,<span><sup><a href="#anie202311460-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202311460-bib-0004_R_d84983799e558" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">2</a></sup></span> electrolyte modification,<span><sup><a href="#anie202311460-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202311460-bib-0011_R_d84983799e561" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">5</a></sup></span> polymerization,<span><sup><a href="#anie202311460-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202311460-bib-0012_R_d84983799e564" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Nat. Commun.">6</a></sup></span> and construction of organic/inorganic composite electrodes.<span><sup><a href="#anie202311460-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202311460-bib-0013_R_d84983799e568" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Chem. Eng. J.">7</a></sup></span> Though colossal improvement has been achieved, and better cycling stabilities were displayed, other concerns have appeared, such as decreased specific capacity or reduced energy density.<span><sup><a href="#anie202311460-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202311460-bib-0014_R_d84983799e571" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8">8</a></sup></span> Therefore, developing OEMs with high specific capacity and good cycling stability is still a considerable challenge.</p>
         
         <p>One of the merits of OEMs is their diverse combination of groups and structures, which allows them to increase the specific capacity and cycling stability simultaneously through functional group modification.<span><sup><a href="#anie202311460-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202311460-bib-0018_R_d84983799e577" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8d - Electrochem. Energy Rev.">8d</a></sup></span> As one of the most promising emerging building units of OEMs, the hexaazatrinaphthalene (HATN) series OEMs display decreased solubility in organic liquid electrolytes due to their rigid, electron-deficient and extended π-conjugated structure, endowing batteries with good cycling stability.<span><sup><a href="#anie202311460-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202311460-bib-0012_R_d84983799e580" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Nat. Commun.">6</a>, <a href="#anie202311460-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202311460-bib-0017_R_d84983799e580" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8c - Adv. Energy Mater.">8c</a>, <a href="#anie202311460-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202311460-bib-0019_R_d84983799e580" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9">9</a></sup></span> Nevertheless, it is reported that two adjacent imine groups could only coordinate with one Na<sup>+</sup> due to the large radius of Na<sup>+</sup>, which restricts the specific capacity of HATN-based OEMs.<span><sup><a href="#anie202311460-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202311460-bib-0012_R_d84983799e587" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Nat. Commun.">6</a>, <a href="#anie202311460-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202311460-bib-0021_R_d84983799e587" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9b - Chem. Eng. J.">9b</a></sup></span> Besides, the extended conjugated structure increases the number of inactive units, reducing the specific capacity of the electrode further. In addition, as a sub-class of carbonyl-based OEMs, catechol (the precursor of <i>ortho</i>-quinone) or <i>ortho</i>-quinone groups possess a high reversible specific capacity in LIBs and SIBs.<span><sup><a href="#anie202311460-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202311460-bib-0022_R_d84983799e595" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10">10</a></sup></span> However, most small-molecule OEMs with catechol/<i>ortho</i>-quinone groups suffer from severe dissolution in organic liquid electrolytes, leading to poor cycling stability.<span><sup><a href="#anie202311460-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202311460-bib-0030_R_d84983799e600" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Adv. Mater.">11</a></sup></span> Therefore, OEMs possess the catechol/<i>ortho</i>-quinone groups and HATN extended conjugated core structures may exhibit both high specific capacity and stable cycling performance in SIBs through synergetic coupling effects of the catechol/<i>ortho</i>-quinone groups and HATN core structure.</p>
         
         <p>Herein, we report the synthesis and application of a heteroaromatic molecule named 2,3,8,9,14,15-hexanol hexaazatrinaphthalene (HATN-6OH), which efficiently combines catechol/<i>ortho</i>-quinone groups and HATN extended conjugated core structures. The abundant redox-active catechol/<i>ortho</i>-quinone and imine groups provide the electrode with a nine-electron transfer mechanism for Na<sup>+</sup> storage and a high theoretical specific capacity of 508 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>. Besides, the synergetic effects of the Na<sup>+</sup> storage pseudocapacitive behavior on catechol/<i>ortho</i>-quinone and the extended conjugated structures of the HATN core benefit a fast charge transfer and electrochemical redox kinetics.<span><sup><a href="#anie202311460-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202311460-bib-0026_R_d84983799e624" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10d - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">10d</a>, <a href="#anie202311460-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202311460-bib-0031_R_d84983799e624" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Nat. Energy">12</a></sup></span> Furthermore, the π–π interactions (HATN core) and intermolecular hydrogen bonds forces (catechol/<i>ortho</i>-quinone groups) among HATN-6OH molecules endow SIBs with stable long-term cycling performance.<span><sup><a href="#anie202311460-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202311460-bib-0032_R_d84983799e629" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13">13</a></sup></span> As a result, the deliberately designed HATN-6OH electrode delivers a high specific capacity of 554 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup>, excellent rate capability (202 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 10 A g<sup>−1</sup>), and stable long-term cycling performance (capacity retention of 73 % after 3000 cycles at 10 A g<sup>−1</sup>). The impressive performance is superior to most reported OEMs for SIBs, including small-molecule and polymer materials. This work demonstrates the importance of the synergetic coupling of different redox-active groups/structures in constructing robust and high-performance OEMs for SIBs and other metal-ion batteries.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="anie202311460-sec-0002">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="anie202311460-sec-0002-title"> Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <p>HATN-6OH is synthesized through four-step chemical reactions according to previous literature with some modifications (more details are shown in Supporting Information, Scheme S1).<span><sup><a href="#anie202311460-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202311460-bib-0036_R_d84983799e652" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">14</a></sup></span> The nuclear magnetic resonance (NMR) spectra confirm the successful synthesis of HATN-6OH and its intermediates (Figure S1–S6 and Figure <a href="#anie202311460-fig-0001">1a</a>–<a href="#anie202311460-fig-0001">b</a>). Two peaks in the <sup>1</sup>H NMR spectrum of HATN-6OH correspond to two types of H protons with different chemical atmospheres of HATN-6OH (Figure <a href="#anie202311460-fig-0001">1a</a>). Moreover, the peak at a chemical shift of about 11.0 ppm is the characteristic signal of the H proton of the catechol group, indicating the successful synthesis of HATN-6OH.<span><sup><a href="#anie202311460-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202311460-bib-0037_R_d84983799e667" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - ACS Energy Lett.">15</a></sup></span> Besides, four peaks in the <sup>13</sup>C NMR spectrum of HATN-6OH consist well with the four types of carbon atoms with different chemical atmospheres of HATN-6OH (Figure <a href="#anie202311460-fig-0001">1b</a>), also confirming the successful synthesis of HATN-6OH. As illustrated in Fourier transform infrared (FTIR) spectroscopy of HATN-6OH (Figure S7), the peaks around 1529 cm<sup>−1</sup> (C=N) and 1396 cm<sup>−1</sup> (C−N) correspond to the peaks of pyrazine rings.<span><sup><a href="#anie202311460-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202311460-bib-0038_R_d84983799e679" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16">16</a></sup></span> Besides, the peak around 1623 cm<sup>−1</sup> indicates the existence of C=O, which is achieved through enolization transformation from catechol groups.<span><sup><a href="#anie202311460-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202311460-bib-0041_R_d84983799e685" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">17</a></sup></span> Furthermore, the strong and broad peak between 3000 and 3500 cm<sup>−1</sup> confirms the existence of −OH groups and intermolecular hydrogen bonding effects among HATN-6OH molecules.<span><sup><a href="#anie202311460-bib-0042" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202311460-bib-0042_R_d84983799e690" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">18</a></sup></span>
            </p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie202311460-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/85753001-7bf4-4048-a98d-ffa8beb6f8ea/anie202311460-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/85753001-7bf4-4048-a98d-ffa8beb6f8ea/anie202311460-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/b8cdd9aa-d925-4caa-b8b7-3784df080963/anie202311460-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/85753001-7bf4-4048-a98d-ffa8beb6f8ea/anie202311460-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202311460-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202311460" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>Chemical and materials characterization of HATN-6OH: (a) <sup>1</sup>H NMR spectrum (400 MHz, DMSO-<i>d</i><sub>6</sub>), (b) <sup>13</sup>C NMR spectrum (400 MHz, DMSO-<i>d</i><sub>6</sub>), (c) SEM image, (d) TEM image, (e) XRD profile and (f) HRTEM image.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>Scanning electron microscopy (SEM) images reveal that HATN-6OH has both nanoparticle and nanorod morphologies (Figure <a href="#anie202311460-fig-0001">1c</a>, Figure S8a and b). This may result from the strong intermolecular hydrogen bonding forces among the HATN-6OH molecules, which further substantiate the FTIR results of HATN-6OH (Figure S7).<span><sup><a href="#anie202311460-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202311460-bib-0026_R_d84983799e732" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10d - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">10d</a>, <a href="#anie202311460-bib-0043" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202311460-bib-0043_R_d84983799e732" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">19</a></sup></span> In addition, transmission electron microscopy (TEM) images of HATN-6OH also display those morphologies (Figure <a href="#anie202311460-fig-0001">1d</a>, Figure S8c and d). These nanorod and nanoparticle structures could facilitate the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion within the HATN-OH electrode and endow SIBs with high-rate capability.<span><sup><a href="#anie202311460-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202311460-bib-0018_R_d84983799e740" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8d - Electrochem. Energy Rev.">8d</a></sup></span> Also, the C, N, and O elemental mapping (Figure S9a–d) is in accordance with the distribution of HATN-6OH materials, demonstrating the successful synthesis of HATN-6OH. The X-ray diffraction (XRD) profile of HATN-6OH exhibits a pronounced peak at around 2<i>θ</i>°=26.5°, corresponding to the (001) plane of HATN-6OH. According to the Bragg equation, the interlayer distance of HATN-6OH is calculated as 0.34 nm, which is the typical value of interlayer distance for organic materials constructed with π–π stacking.<span><sup><a href="#anie202311460-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202311460-bib-0031_R_d84983799e746" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Nat. Energy">12</a>, <a href="#anie202311460-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202311460-bib-0032_R_d84983799e746" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13">13</a>, <a href="#anie202311460-bib-0044" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202311460-bib-0044_R_d84983799e746" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20">20</a></sup></span> Besides, high-resolution TEM (HRTEM) shows the layer-by-layer π–π stacking with noticeable crystal lattice fringes (Figure <a href="#anie202311460-fig-0001">1f</a> and Figure S10). And the crystal lattice fringes spacing is about 0.34 nm, which consists well with the interlayer distance calculated by XRD results. This layer-by-layer π–π stacking of HATN-6OH could facilitate Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion into the inner space of HATN-6OH and ultimately benefit the cycling stability and rate capability of SIBs.<span><sup><a href="#anie202311460-bib-0048" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202311460-bib-0048_R_d84983799e754" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Chem. Commun.">21</a></sup></span></p>
         
         <p>In addition, the nitrogen adsorption/desorption characterization is conducted to measure the micro-structure of HATN-6OH. The Brunauer–Emmett–Teller (BET) surface area of HATN-6OH is 42 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>−1</sup> (Figure S11a), and the dominant pore size of HATN-6OH is around 11 nm (Figure S11b), indicating that most pores are mesopores. Furthermore, the thermogravimetric analysis (TGA) is conducted after annealing the sample at 120 °C for 24 h under a vacuum to characterize the thermal stability of HATN-6OH (Figure S12). The result shows that only 7.5 % weight loss is detected under 150 °C, which may result from the trace of water molecules adsorbed by HATN-OH due to the high hygroscopicity of HATN-6OH (catechol groups) and nanostructure. After that, the weight loss of HATN-6OH over 300 °C may result from the decomposition of fringe groups such as catechol/ortho-quinone groups of HATN-6OH. Therefore, HATN-6OH is stable enough for SIBs application because the usual work temperature of SIBs is lower than 80 °C. The good thermal stability of HATN-6OH may stem from the HATN extended conjugated core structures, π–π interaction, and strong intermolecular hydrogen bonding among HATN-6OH.<span><sup><a href="#anie202311460-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202311460-bib-0031_R_d84983799e763" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Nat. Energy">12</a>, <a href="#anie202311460-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202311460-bib-0034_R_d84983799e763" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13b - Adv. Mater.">13b</a>, <a href="#anie202311460-bib-0049" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202311460-bib-0049_R_d84983799e763" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">22</a></sup></span></p>
         
         <p>The electrochemical properties of the as-prepared HATN-6OH electrodes in SIBs are investigated firstly by cyclic voltammetry (CV). Figure <a href="#anie202311460-fig-0002">2a</a> displays the CV curves of the first four cycles of HATN-6OH in 0.01–3.0 V (vs. Na<sup>+</sup>/Na) at a scan rate of 0.1 mV s<sup>−1</sup>. The CV curve of HATN-6OH is composed of three parts: the flat CV curve in the range from 1.25 to 3.0 V could be attributed to the Na<sup>+</sup> bonding/de-bonding with catechol/<i>ortho</i>-quinone groups, a pair of redox peaks (0.85 V/0.76 V) may derive from the interactions between C=N and Na<sup>+</sup>, a pair of redox peaks (0.44 V/0.24 V) may be assigned to the interactions between catechol/<i>ortho</i>-quinone with another Na<sup>+</sup>.<span><sup><a href="#anie202311460-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202311460-bib-0021_R_d84983799e787" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9b - Chem. Eng. J.">9b</a>, <a href="#anie202311460-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202311460-bib-0023_R_d84983799e787" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10a - Energy Environ. Sci.">10a</a>, <a href="#anie202311460-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202311460-bib-0026_R_d84983799e787" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10d - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">10d</a></sup></span> The broader CV curve of the first cycle of HATN-6OH than the subsequent CV curve is derived from the formation of solid electrolyte interphase (SEI) on the electrode.<span><sup><a href="#anie202311460-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202311460-bib-0011_R_d84983799e790" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">5</a></sup></span> The CV curves of subsequent cycles are overlapped with each other, demonstrating the good electrochemical reversibility of HATN-6OH in SIBs.<span><sup><a href="#anie202311460-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202311460-bib-0016_R_d84983799e793" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8b - Adv. Energy Mater.">8b</a></sup></span> Besides, the charge–discharge profiles of the first four cycles of HATN-6OH at 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup> are illustrated in Figure <a href="#anie202311460-fig-0002">2b</a>. The charge–discharge profiles of the following cycles of HATN-6OH are also overlapped.
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie202311460-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/fd202f82-9921-48ad-8cd8-8529edbf3a42/anie202311460-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/fd202f82-9921-48ad-8cd8-8529edbf3a42/anie202311460-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/57516de9-8d0f-49d7-9d48-bb2a35b0cd20/anie202311460-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/fd202f82-9921-48ad-8cd8-8529edbf3a42/anie202311460-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202311460-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202311460" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>The electrochemical evaluation of the as-prepared HATN-6OH electrode: (a) CV curves at 0.1 mV s<sup>−1</sup>, (b) charge–discharge profiles at 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup>, (c) rate capabilities, (d) comparison of the rate capabilities with other organic anodes in SIBs, (e) long-term cycling performance.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>The rate capability and corresponding charge–discharge profiles of the HATN-6OH electrodes are then characterized at stepwise current densities of 0.1, 0.2, 0.5, 1, 2, 5 and 10 A g<sup>−1</sup> (Figure <a href="#anie202311460-fig-0002">2c</a> and Figure S13), which possess the specific capacity of 554, 533, 471, 379, 320, 254 and 202 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. The high specific capacity over 550 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> is derived from abundant redox-active catechol/<i>ortho</i>-quinone and imine groups of the HATN-6OH molecule. Most importantly, the rate capability of the HATN-6OH electrode at an extreme current density of 10 A g<sup>−1</sup> is much higher than most OEMs, including both small-molecule and polymer materials for SIBs (Figure <a href="#anie202311460-fig-0002">2d</a> and Table S1).<span><sup><a href="#anie202311460-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202311460-bib-0004_R_d84983799e849" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">2</a>, <a href="#anie202311460-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202311460-bib-0011_R_d84983799e849" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">5</a>, <a href="#anie202311460-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202311460-bib-0026_R_d84983799e849" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10d - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">10d</a>, <a href="#anie202311460-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202311460-bib-0041_R_d84983799e849" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">17</a>, <a href="#anie202311460-bib-0050" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202311460-bib-0050_R_d84983799e849" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23">23</a></sup></span> The outstanding rate capability of the HATN-6OH electrode is attributed to the fast pseudocapacitive electrochemical redox kinetics of catechol/<i>ortho</i>-quinone units and extended π-conjugated structures. Furthermore, the long-term cycling performance of the HATN-6OH electrode at a high current density of 10 A g<sup>−1</sup> is illustrated in Figure <a href="#anie202311460-fig-0002">2e</a>. After 3000 cycles, the electrode still retains a specific capacity of 155 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> with a high-capacity retention of 73 %, superior to most reported OEMs characterized at such high current densities (Table S1). In addition, the HATN-6OH electrode after 3000 cycles exhibits similar morphology with that before 3000 cycles (Figure S14), which also confirms the robust cycling stability of HATN-6OH electrode. The stable cycling performance of the HATN-6OH electrode could also result from the synergetic coupling of the HATN extended conjugated core structures, π–π interactions, and strong intermolecular hydrogen interforce among the HATN-6OH molecules.<span><sup><a href="#anie202311460-bib-0049" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202311460-bib-0049_R_d84983799e862" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">22</a>, <a href="#anie202311460-bib-0058" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202311460-bib-0058_R_d84983799e862" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Acc. Chem. Res.">24</a></sup></span></p>
         
         <div class="paragraph-element">Then we further investigate the electrochemical reaction kinetics of the HATN-6OH electrode. The CV curves at various scan rates of 0.1, 0.2, 0.5, 1, and 2 mV s<sup>−1</sup> are illustrated in Figure <a href="#anie202311460-fig-0003">3a</a>. The area of CV curve shapes increases with the rise of scan rates, illustrating more significant polarizations at high scan rates.<span><sup><a href="#anie202311460-bib-0059" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202311460-bib-0059_R_d84983799e872" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">25</a></sup></span> According to previous research, the relationship between the peak current (<i>i</i>) and scan rate (<i>v</i>) can be illustrated as:<span><sup><a href="#anie202311460-bib-0060" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202311460-bib-0060_R_d84983799e880" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">26</a></sup></span><div class="inline-equation" id="anie202311460-disp-0001"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><img class="" src="/cms/asset/fcd1acae-190c-4319-82f9-5f36145648d3/anie202311460-math-0001.png" alt="urn:x-wiley:14337851:media:anie202311460:anie202311460-math-0001" title="urn:x-wiley:14337851:media:anie202311460:anie202311460-math-0001" loading="lazy"></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(1)</span></div>
            </div>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie202311460-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/c1a4d479-b710-4f45-94ce-816599a14a42/anie202311460-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/c1a4d479-b710-4f45-94ce-816599a14a42/anie202311460-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/36eb875b-1c92-4b89-8439-ed2a39b8fbdd/anie202311460-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/c1a4d479-b710-4f45-94ce-816599a14a42/anie202311460-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202311460-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202311460" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>The analysis of electrochemical reaction kinetics of the HATN-6OH electrode: (a) CV curves of HATN-6OH at various scan rates, (b) log(<i>i</i><sub>peak</sub>) versus log(<i>v</i>), (c) total current and current ratio from capacitive-controlled process at a scan rate of 2 mV s<sup>−1</sup>, (d) capacitive contribution ratios at different scan rates.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>the values of <i>b</i> could be calculated after plotting log (<i>i</i>) against log (<i>v</i>) (Figure <a href="#anie202311460-fig-0003">3b</a>), which represents the electrochemical reaction kinetics of the Na<sup>+</sup> storage by the HATN-6OH electrode.<span><sup><a href="#anie202311460-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202311460-bib-0012_R_d84983799e934" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Nat. Commun.">6</a></sup></span> When the value of <i>b</i> is close to 0.5, the Na<sup>+</sup> storage behavior belongs to a diffusion-controlled process (DCP). If the value of <i>b</i> is close to 1, the Na<sup>+</sup> storage behavior is dominated by a capacitive-controlled process (CCP). The calculated <i>b</i> values for peaks 1 and 2 in Figure <a href="#anie202311460-fig-0003">3a</a> are 0.87 and 1.00 (Figure <a href="#anie202311460-fig-0003">3b</a>), indicating that the capacitive-controlled process dominates the Na<sup>+</sup> storage in the HATN-6OH electrode,<span><sup><a href="#anie202311460-bib-0061" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202311460-bib-0061_R_d84983799e956" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Adv. Energy Mater.">27</a></sup></span> which is attributed to the fast redox reaction kinetics of catechol/<i>ortho</i>-quinone units and extended π-conjugated core structures.<span><sup><a href="#anie202311460-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202311460-bib-0024_R_d84983799e961" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10b - Adv. Mater.">10b</a>, <a href="#anie202311460-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202311460-bib-0026_R_d84983799e961" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10d - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">10d</a>, <a href="#anie202311460-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202311460-bib-0031_R_d84983799e961" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Nat. Energy">12</a>, <a href="#anie202311460-bib-0062" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202311460-bib-0062_R_d84983799e961" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Nat. Energy">28</a></sup></span></p>
         
         <div class="paragraph-element">To further quantitatively determine the role of the capacitive-controlled process in the electrochemical process, the total current (<i>i</i>) could be divided into the current contributed by the capacitive-controlled process and diffusion-controlled process through the following equation:<span><sup><a href="#anie202311460-bib-0063" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202311460-bib-0063_R_d84983799e969" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">29</a></sup></span><div class="inline-equation" id="anie202311460-disp-0002"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><img class="" src="/cms/asset/9592c027-6429-4397-89d0-0bc98d04489b/anie202311460-math-0002.png" alt="urn:x-wiley:14337851:media:anie202311460:anie202311460-math-0002" title="urn:x-wiley:14337851:media:anie202311460:anie202311460-math-0002" loading="lazy"></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(2)</span></div>
            </div>
         
         <p>where <i>k</i><sub>1</sub> and <i>k</i><sub>2</sub> are parameters, and <i>k</i><sub>1</sub><i>v</i> and <i>k</i><sub>2</sub><i>v</i><sup>1/2</sup> indicate the current contributed by the capacitive and diffusion-controlled process, respectively. The current contribution ratio from the capacitive-controlled process at a scan rate of 2 mV s<sup>−1</sup> is calculated at around 86 % (Figure <a href="#anie202311460-fig-0003">3c</a>). The other capacitive-controlled current contribution ratios at different scan rates are illustrated in Figure S15 and Figure <a href="#anie202311460-fig-0003">3d</a>, indicating larger ratios of the capacitive-controlled current contribution at higher scan rates.<span><sup><a href="#anie202311460-bib-0059" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202311460-bib-0059_R_d84983799e1006" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">25</a></sup></span> In addition, the high contribution ratio from the capacitive-controlled process discloses the reason for the excellent rate capability of the HATN-6OH electrode discussed above.</p>
         
         <p>To understand the impressive electrochemical performance of the HATN-6OH electrode, we shift our focus to investigate the Na<sup>+</sup> storage mechanism of the HATN-6OH in SIBs. Electrodes at five stages (a: pristine, b: the 1st discharge to 0.01 V, c: the 1st recharge to 3.0 V, d: the 2nd discharge to 0.01 V, e: the 2nd recharge to 3.0 V) of the first and second charge/discharge cycles curves are selected to investigate the Na<sup>+</sup> storage mechanism (Figure <a href="#anie202311460-fig-0004">4a</a>). Firstly, ATR-FTIR is utilized to examine the variation of polar groups (C=O, C−O, C=N, C−N) of HATN-6OH during the charge/discharge process. As illustrated in Figure <a href="#anie202311460-fig-0004">4b</a>, the transmittance intensity ratio between the carbonyl group (C=O) at 1646 cm<sup>−1</sup> and C−O group at 1227 cm<sup>−1</sup> of the HATN-6OH electrode after discharging to 0.01 V is lower than that of the pristine HATN-6OH electrode, indicating the decreased quantity of carbonyl groups and the increased quantity of C−O groups.<span><sup><a href="#anie202311460-bib-0064" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202311460-bib-0064_R_d84983799e1027" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30">30</a></sup></span> This result is attributed to two reasons: first, some carbonyl groups that existed in HATN-6OH (as shown in Figure S7) could transform into C−O groups after chelating with Na<sup>+</sup>, leading to the decreased quantity of the carbonyl groups and the increased quantity of the C−O groups in the HATN-6OH electrode, ultimately.<span><sup><a href="#anie202311460-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202311460-bib-0011_R_d84983799e1032" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">5</a>, <a href="#anie202311460-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202311460-bib-0026_R_d84983799e1032" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10d - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">10d</a></sup></span> Besides, the catechol groups are also capable of chelating with Na<sup>+</sup> and converting to C−O groups after discharging to a low potential (0.01 V herein).<span><sup><a href="#anie202311460-bib-0067" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202311460-bib-0067_R_d84983799e1037" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31">31</a></sup></span> When the HATN-6OH electrode is recharged to 3.0 V, the transmittance intensity ratio between the C=O group and C−O group of the HATN-6OH electrode is increased in comparison with that of the HATN-6OH electrode after discharging to 0.01 V, implying the increased amount of C=O groups and the decrease of C−O groups in the HATN-6OH electrode.<span><sup><a href="#anie202311460-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202311460-bib-0017_R_d84983799e1041" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8c - Adv. Energy Mater.">8c</a>, <a href="#anie202311460-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202311460-bib-0039_R_d84983799e1041" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16a - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">16a</a>, <a href="#anie202311460-bib-0071" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202311460-bib-0071_R_d84983799e1041" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - J. Mater. Chem. A">32</a></sup></span> And the variation of transmittance intensity ratio between C=O and C−O groups in the second discharge/charge cycle is similar to the first discharge/charge cycle, demonstrating the good electrochemical reversibility of C=O and C−O groups. In addition, the transmittance intensity ratio between the imine group (C=N) at 1515 cm<sup>−1</sup> and the C−N group at 1368 cm<sup>−1</sup> of the HATN-6OH electrode after discharging to 0.01 V is lower than that of the pristine HATN-6OH electrode, indicating the decreased amount of the C=N groups and the increased amount of the C−N groups of HATN-6OH through coordination between the C=N groups and Na<sup>+</sup> after discharging to 0.01 V.<span><sup><a href="#anie202311460-bib-0072" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202311460-bib-0072_R_d84983799e1050" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33">33</a></sup></span> When the HATN-6OH electrode is recharged to 3.00 V, the transmittance intensity ratio between the C=N and C−N groups is increased, implying the transformation from the C−N to C=N groups.<span><sup><a href="#anie202311460-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202311460-bib-0039_R_d84983799e1053" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16a - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">16a</a></sup></span> Furthermore, the redox groups variation of the C=N and C−N of the HATN-6OH electrode in the second cycle is similar to that of the first cycle, also illustrating good electrochemical reversibility between the C=N and C−N groups. These results confirm that both the C=O and C=N groups are redox-active groups and have good reversibility in the electrochemical process in SIBs.
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie202311460-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/63a44330-4dc3-4350-8013-7ca5d69ffaf5/anie202311460-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/63a44330-4dc3-4350-8013-7ca5d69ffaf5/anie202311460-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/ccc93d96-a5be-4898-a486-f0a063dba5ea/anie202311460-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/63a44330-4dc3-4350-8013-7ca5d69ffaf5/anie202311460-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202311460-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202311460" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>The investigation of the Na<sup>+</sup> storage mechanism of HATN-6OH: (a) charge/discharge curves of the first and second cycles (a: pristine, b: 1st discharge to 0.01 V, c: 1st charge to 3.0 V, d: 2nd discharge to 0.01 V, e: 2nd charge to 3.0 V), (b) ex situ ATR-FTIR of HATN-6OH electrode during the first and second charge/discharge cycles, (c) Raman spectroscopies, the ESP map of (d) HATN-6C=O and (e) HATN-6 C-ONa, (f) the sodiation behavior from simulation (the left axis is the voltage vs. Na<sup>+</sup>/Na, the right axis is the total energy of various sodiation degree of HATN-6C=O), (g) molecule structural evolution during sodiation and desodiation process (deep brown ball: C; blue ball: N; little brown ball: H; red ball: O; yellow ball: Na<sup>+</sup>).</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>During the charge/discharge process, non-polar groups such as the C−C, C=C, and benzene rings have also changed, accompanied by the variation of polar groups, which could be investigated by Raman spectroscopy due to their high Raman activity,<span><sup><a href="#anie202311460-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202311460-bib-0027_R_d84983799e1088" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10e - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">10e</a></sup></span> and the related characterizations are shown in Figure <a href="#anie202311460-fig-0004">4c</a>. Two pronounced peaks at around 1311 and 1595 cm<sup>−1</sup> are attributed to the D and G bands, respectively.<span><sup><a href="#anie202311460-bib-0076" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202311460-bib-0076_R_d84983799e1096" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">34</a></sup></span> The D band originates from the <i>sp</i><sup>3</sup> C and bent <i>sp</i><sup>2</sup> C, and the G band stems from the conjugated <i>sp</i><sup>2</sup> C (the C=C on the benzene ring herein).<span><sup><a href="#anie202311460-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202311460-bib-0012_R_d84983799e1109" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Nat. Commun.">6</a></sup></span> The D band displays a downward shift due to the increased aromaticity of the C=C groups on the benzene ring during the discharge process, which accompany the upward growth of the G band owing to the increased quantity of C=C (accompanying with the transformation from <i>ortho</i>-quinone to C-ONa and C=N to C−N by coordinating with Na<sup>+</sup>).<span><sup><a href="#anie202311460-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202311460-bib-0027_R_d84983799e1117" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10e - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">10e</a></sup></span> On the contrary, the D band displays an upward shift, and the G band has a downward shift during the charging process. Overall, the five points during the first and second cycles exhibit a periodic intensity ratio of the D and G band (<i>I</i><sub>D</sub>/<i>I</i><sub>G</sub>) as 1.37, 1.09, 1.45, 1.12, 1.47, respectively. The Raman results consist well with the ATR-FTIR data, and both of them confirm that <i>ortho</i>-quinone and imine groups are redox-active centers during charge/discharge process.</p>
         
         <p>Taking all the ex situ ATR-FTIR and Raman experimental results into consideration, the final reversible redox-active group pairs are C=O/C−ONa and C=N/C−NNa.<span><sup><a href="#anie202311460-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202311460-bib-0024_R_d84983799e1131" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10b - Adv. Mater.">10b</a>, <a href="#anie202311460-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202311460-bib-0026_R_d84983799e1131" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10d - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">10d</a>, <a href="#anie202311460-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202311460-bib-0031_R_d84983799e1131" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Nat. Energy">12</a>, <a href="#anie202311460-bib-0046" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202311460-bib-0046_R_d84983799e1131" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20b - Adv. Funct. Mater.">20b</a></sup></span> Therefore, a HATN-6C=O (i.e., fully recharged state of HATN-6OH) is utilized to obtain insight into the Na<sup>+</sup> storage mechanism and molecular structure evolution during the sodiation and desodiation process by DFT calculation. Firstly, the DFT calculation is used to simulate the electrostatic potential (ESP) maps of HATN-6C=O (Figure <a href="#anie202311460-fig-0004">4d</a>), which shows that most <i>ortho</i>-quinone and imine groups are electron-rich and redox-active regions.<span><sup><a href="#anie202311460-bib-0077" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202311460-bib-0077_R_d84983799e1141" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">35</a></sup></span> And according to the DFT calculation, the highest number of Na<sup>+</sup> coordinated by HATN-6C=O is nine because Na<sup>+</sup> will prefer to become Na metal instead of bonding with HATN-6C=O if the number of Na<sup>+</sup> is higher than nine. After bonding with 9 Na<sup>+</sup>, these electron-rich areas of HATN-6C=O lose electrons and become red in the ESP map of HATN-6C-ONa (Figure <a href="#anie202311460-fig-0004">4e</a>), corresponding to the fully sodiation state with the theoretical capacity of 508 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>.<span><sup><a href="#anie202311460-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202311460-bib-0012_R_d84983799e1159" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Nat. Commun.">6</a>, <a href="#anie202311460-bib-0078" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202311460-bib-0078_R_d84983799e1159" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - Adv. Energy Mater.">36</a></sup></span> According to our experimental results, the specific capacity of the HATN-6C=O electrode (including active materials and super P) is 554 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup> (Figure <a href="#anie202311460-fig-0002">2c</a>), and the specific capacity contribution from Super P in the HATN-6C=O electrode can be calculated as 151 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> (Figure S16). Therefore, the practical specific capacity from HATN-6C=O material is 403 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, which is equal to 79.3 % of the theoretical specific capacity of HATN-6C=O.</p>
         
         <p>The detailed sodiation process could be explained in Figures <a href="#anie202311460-fig-0004">4f</a> and 4 g, which could be divided into three steps in general. Firstly, one <i>ortho</i>-quinone group chelates one Na<sup>+</sup>; therefore, three <i>ortho</i>-quinone groups of HATN-6C=O bonding with three Na<sup>+</sup> accompanying the energy difference of −1.33 eV. Then, an extra three Na<sup>+</sup> could bond with three imine groups with an energy difference of −1.32 eV. At last, three <i>ortho</i>-quinone groups could bond another three Na<sup>+</sup> with an energy difference of −1.33 eV. For the charging process, the Na<sup>+</sup> de-bonding sequence can be reversed to the above discharge process (Figure <a href="#anie202311460-fig-0004">4g</a>). Therefore, the DFT calculations confirm and complement the experimental results, demonstrating that the nine Na<sup>+</sup> storage mechanisms of HATN-6C=O and <i>ortho</i>-quinone and imine groups are redox-active centers.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="anie202311460-sec-0003">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="anie202311460-sec-0003-title"> Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>In summary, a heteroaromatic molecule (i.e., HATN-6OH) was designed and synthesized as high specific capacity and stable electrochemical Na<sup>+</sup> storage OEM due to the synergetic effects of catechol/<i>ortho</i>-quinone groups, HATN extended conjugated core structures, π–π interactions, and strong intermolecular hydrogen interforce. The as-prepared HATN-6OH electrode displayed a high specific capacity of 554 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1 mA g<sup>−1</sup>, excellent rate capability (202 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 10 A g<sup>−1</sup>) and stable long-term cycling performance (73 % after 3000 cycles at 10 A g<sup>−1</sup>). Furthermore, ATR-FTIR, Raman, and DFT calculations confirmed that <i>ortho</i>-quinone and imine groups are redox-active sites during the cycling process. This work not only presents a promising OEM with high specific capacity and stable cycling performance but also demonstrates the importance of synergetic coupling of redox-active sites of different groups/structures for advanced SIBs and other metal-ion batteries.</p>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">
